النسبة
العرض
المحال
العقلي
الفرضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That relational attributes like 'above-ness' are real accidents existing in the object.
Correction: Razi shows that since the essence remains unchanged when the relation changes, the relation cannot be a real accident; it is merely a conceptual relation.

Checkpoint
What does the argument about the body and above-ness demonstrate?
A) That 'above-ness' is a real accident of the body.
B) That the essence changes when the relation changes.
C) That the relation 'above-ness' is not a real accident because the essence remains the same.
D) That relations are impossible.

Key Takeaway
Relational attributes like 'above-ness' are not real accidents because the essence remains unchanged when the relation changes; they are mental concepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tied your sense of self-worth to a changing relation—like a job title, a friendship, or someone's opinion—and felt shaken when it shifted? How does Razi's argument that such relations are not real accidents in your essence free you from that instability? What would it look like this week to hold your essence as unchanging and let relations be mere mental concepts?
?
When you make a mistake or lose a status, do you feel that *you* have changed at a fundamental level? Razi insists the essence remains the same despite changes in relations. Can you identify one relational attribute (e.g., 'respected', 'wealthy', 'successful') that you treat as if it were part of your identity? What would it mean to consciously detach your core self from that label starting today?

Divine Attributes

Muammar and the Problem of Infinite Accidents
The mutakallimun refute Muammar's infinite accidents by arguing that every number has a finite half.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the view that infinite accidents can exist leads to contradiction, and how Muammar attempted to defend it.

Core Idea
If every number has a half, an infinite series would be finite.

Muammar, an early mutakallim, accepted the reality of relational attributes and argued that they form an infinite chain of accidents. The mainstream mutakallimun rejected this, using an argument based on halves: every existing number has a half, and if the series is infinite, its half would also be infinite, leading to contradiction. Muammar countered by denying that every number has a half and by appealing to the concept of smaller infinities. In this lesson, we study this exchange and the marginal clarification on infinite sets.

Guided Path
1
Read the exchange between Muammar and the mutakallimun
The Arabic presents Muammar's acceptance, the mutakallimun's objection, and Muammar's reply. Read through carefully to see the dialectic.
2
Analyze the argument from halves
The mutakallimun argue: every existing number has a half; the half is less than the whole; whatever is less than another is finite; therefore the half is finite; and any number with a finite half is itself finite. So no infinite number of accidents can exist.
Note the implicit assumption that numbers are finite.
3
Understand Muammar's counter and Razi's clarification
Muammar challenges both premises: not every number has a half (only finite numbers do), and being less than another does not imply finitude (e.g., Allah's powers are infinite but less than His knowledge). Razi's marginal note explains that the infinite does not become finite by subtraction, and one infinite can be half of another without becoming finite.
This anticipates later discussions of actual infinites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في نفس الذات، لأن الجسم من حيث إنه جسم غير مقول بالقياس إلى الغير ، ومن حيث إنه فوق مقول بالقياس إلى الغير، ولأن الشيء قد لا يكون فوق ثم يصير فوقا فالذات باقية فى الحالين والفوقية غير حاصلة في الحالين (۱)
•
ثم إن معمراً من قدماء المتكلمين أثبت لقوة هذه الحجة هذه الأغراض النسبية ولم يجد دافعا للتسلسلات المذكورة فالتزمها وأثبت أعراضا لا نهاية لها يقوم كل واحد منها بالآخر . وقال المتكلمون : هذا باطل، لأن كل عدد موجود فله نصف ونصفه أقل من كله ، وكلما كان أقل من غيره فهو متناه فنصفه متناه في العدد ، وكل ما نصفه متناه فكله متناه لأنه ضعف المتناهي
قال معمر : لانسلم أن كل عدد فله نصف بل ذلك من خواص العدد المتناهى ، سلمنا لكن لم قلت بأن كل ما كان أقل من غيره فهو متناه ، أليس أن مقدورات الله تعالى أقل من معلوماته وتضعيف الألف مرارا لانهاية لها أقل من تضعيف الألفين مرارا لانهاية لها (۲)
ونحن نقول : حجة الفلاسفة على إثبات النسب يقتضى كون المتقدم والمتأخر صفتين موجودتين وذلك محال ، لأن الإضافتين توجدان معا ومحلاهما يوجدان معا فالقبل موجود مع البعد هذا خلف، ولأنا نحكم على اليوم الماضى فى اليوم الحاضر بكونه ماضيا والمفهوم من كونه ماضيا ليس أمرا سلبيا لأنه صار ماضيا بعد ما لم يكن ماضيا فإذا هو ثبوتى ، وليس ثبوته في الذهن فقط ، فإنا لو فرضنا عدم الفرض والاعتبار فذلك اليوم ماض في نفسه وليس عبارة عن نفس ذلك اليوم ، لأنه حين كان حاضرا لم يكن ماضيا ، فيلزم أن يكون وصف كونه ماضيا عرضا حقيقيا قائما به حال عدمه فيكون الموجود قائما بالمعدوم وهو محال (۳)
(۱) أقول : كون الشيء عقليا كفوقية السماء يباين كونه فرضيا، فإن تحتية السماء ربما يفرض ، بل المقلى الذى يجب أن يحدث فى العقل إذا عقل العقل ذلك الشيء كفوقية السماء . وأما الفرضي فهو الذي يفرضه الفارض وإن كان محالا والذهن يشتملهما ، ويجب أن يفهم كل واحد منهما لئلا يقع بسبب الاشتباه غلط (۲) أقول : غير المتناهى لايصير متناهيا بنقصان كل شيء منه ، والتي ربما يكون متناهيا من وجه غير من وجه فلحقه خواص المتناهى من الوجه الأول وخواص غير المتناهى من الوجه الآخر ، وهذا كتضعيف الألف والألفين مرارا لانهاية لها فيكون أحد غير المتناهيين نصفا الآخر ، ولا يلزمه منه تناهى أحدهما. (۳) أقول : قد بينا أن الإضافة تعقل عند تصور المضافين والمتقدم والمتأخر موجودان في التصور معا ولا يلزم ذلك قيام موجود معدوم ، بل يلزم حدوث معقول متصور وذلك غير محال ، وقد عرفت أن ذلك ثابت فى نفس الأمر من غير الفرض وليس بالذهن الصرف.

(1) Then Muammar, an early mutakallim, affirmed these relative accidents due to the strength of this argument and found no way to avoid the mentioned infinite regresses, so he accepted them and affirmed infinite accidents, each subsisting in another. The mutakallimun said: This is false, because every existing number has a half, and its half is less than the whole, and whatever is less than another is finite, so its half is finite in number, and whatever has a finite half is itself finite because it is double the finite. Muammar said: We do not concede that every number has a half; rather that is a property of finite numbers. Granted, but why did you say that whatever is less than another is finite? Are not Allah's powers less than His knowledge, and the doubling of a thousand an infinite number of times less than the doubling of two thousand an infinite number of times? (2) And we say: The philosophers' proof for affirming relations requires that the prior and the posterior be two existent attributes, and that is impossible, because the two relations exist together and their loci exist together, so the prior would exist with the posterior – this is a contradiction. And because we judge of yesterday in the present day that it is past, and the concept of its being past is not a negative matter because it became past after it was not past, so it is positive. And its positivity is not only in the mind, for if we assume the absence of assumption and consideration, that day is past in itself and is not the same as that day because when it was present it was not past. So it follows that the description of its being past is a real accident subsisting in it at the time of its non-existence, so an existent would subsist in a non-existent, which is impossible. (3) (1) I say: The thing being 'aqli like the above-ness of the sky differs from its being fardi; for the below-ness of the sky may be assumed. Rather the 'aqli is what must occur in the intellect when the intellect cognizes that thing, like the above-ness of the sky. As for the fardi, it is what the assumer assumes even if it is impossible, and the mind encompasses both. Each must be understood to avoid error due to confusion. (2) I say: The infinite does not become finite by subtracting something from it, and it may be finite in one respect and infinite in another, so it has the properties of the finite in the first respect and the properties of the infinite in the other respect. This is like the doubling of a thousand and two thousand an infinite number of times, so one of the two infinites is half of the other, and it does not necessitate the finitude of either. And you have known that this is established in the very matter without assumption, and it is not merely mental.

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument. First, a proof is given that relational attributes like 'above-ness' are not real existent attributes because the essence remains unchanged while the relation changes. Second, Muammar's objection is presented: he accepted infinite accidents to avoid infinite regress. The mutakallimun refute him by arguing that every finite number has a half, and an infinite series would not have such halves. Muammar counters that not every number has a half, and that smaller infinities exist. Third, the author presents a direct argument against the philosophers' proof for relations, showing that it would require an accident to subsist in a non-existent (like the past day being described as past while it no longer exists), which is impossible. The marginal notes clarify key concepts: the distinction between 'aqli (intellectual) and fardi (assumptive) entities; the nature of the infinite (that one infinite can be half of another without becoming finite); and the defense that relations are only conceived in the mind and do not imply real accidents subsisting in non-existents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That an infinite series cannot be compared as half and whole because that would imply finitude.
Correction: Razi shows that one infinite can be half of another infinite without either becoming finite, as in the doubling of a thousand vs. two thousand infinite times.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection of the mutakallimun to Muammar's view that there can be infinite accidents?
A) Infinite regresses are impossible.
B) Every existing number has a half, and a half is finite, so the whole must be finite.
C) Infinite accidents would cause contradiction between prior and posterior.
D) Relational attributes cannot be infinite.

Key Takeaway
The debate between Muammar and the mutakallimun reveals the need to clarify the nature of the infinite and whether an actual infinite can exist in reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the mutakallimun's argument that every number has a half, does it unsettle you to realize that Allah's attributes are infinite yet one can be 'greater' than another? How does this intellectual tension affect your understanding of His perfections? Take a moment to articulate what this means for your own relationship with the divine attributes.
?
Muammar and the mutakallimun both sought to understand the nature of reality through rational argument. Have you ever felt a conflict between what reason seems to demand and what you have been taught about Allah? How can you reconcile intellectual honesty with faith? Write down one area where this tension arises in your own heart.
?
The debate here hinges on whether an actual infinite can exist. Consider the infinite mercy of Allah and your finite sins. Do you truly believe that His mercy is infinite in a way that covers your every wrong, or do you secretly think some sin is too great? Let this lesson's discussion on the nature of infinity challenge you to trust in the boundlessness of His forgiveness today.

The Philosophers' Proof and the Absurdity of a Past-Day Accident
Razi argues that the philosophers' proof for relations leads to an accident subsisting in a non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the philosophers' proof for relations leads to the absurdity of an accident subsisting in a non-existent.

Core Idea
Treating 'pastness' as a real accident would mean a quality subsists in nothing.

Razi now turns to a direct critique of the philosophers' argument that relations like priority and posteriority are real things. He shows that if 'being past' were a real accident, it would have to exist in the past day even when that day no longer exists, which is impossible. The marginal note then explains that relations are merely mental and do not imply real accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
This part contains two arguments: (1) the prior and posterior would have to exist together, which is contradictory; (2) the example of yesterday being past shows an accident subsisting in a non-existent.
2
Understand the first argument: prior and posterior coexisting
If priority and posteriority are real accidents, then the prior and the posterior would need to exist simultaneously because relations are simultaneous. But by definition, the prior is before the posterior, so they cannot coexist. This is a contradiction.
Note that the argument assumes that the relata (prior and posterior) must exist for the relation to be real.
3
Analyze the second argument: yesterday being past
We say yesterday is past. The concept of 'past' is positive because it became past after not being past. This positivity cannot be only in the mind, because even without any mind, yesterday is past in itself. Therefore, 'pastness' would be a real accident of yesterday. But yesterday no longer exists, so the accident would subsist in a non-existent, which is absurd.
This is a classic argument against the reality of relations.
4
Read Razi's marginal clarification
Razi explains that relations are conceived when the two relata are conceived together. They do not exist as real accidents but as intelligible concepts. Thus, there is no accident subsisting in a non-existent.
This resolves the philosophers' proof by showing that relations are mental, not real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

•

قال معمر : لانسلم أن كل عدد فله نصف بل ذلك من خواص العدد المتناهى ، سلمنا لكن لم قلت بأن كل ما كان أقل من غيره فهو متناه ، أليس أن مقدورات الله تعالى أقل من معلوماته وتضعيف الألف مرارا لانهاية لها أقل من تضعيف الألفين مرارا لانهاية لها (۲)
ونحن نقول : حجة الفلاسفة على إثبات النسب يقتضى كون المتقدم والمتأخر صفتين موجودتين وذلك محال ، لأن الإضافتين توجدان معا ومحلاهما يوجدان معا فالقبل موجود مع البعد هذا خلف، ولأنا نحكم على اليوم الماضى فى اليوم الحاضر بكونه ماضيا والمفهوم من كونه ماضيا ليس أمرا سلبيا لأنه صار ماضيا بعد ما لم يكن ماضيا فإذا هو ثبوتى ، وليس ثبوته في الذهن فقط ، فإنا لو فرضنا عدم الفرض والاعتبار فذلك اليوم ماض في نفسه وليس عبارة عن نفس ذلك اليوم ، لأنه حين كان حاضرا لم يكن ماضيا ، فيلزم أن يكون وصف كونه ماضيا عرضا حقيقيا قائما به حال عدمه فيكون الموجود قائما بالمعدوم وهو محال (۳)
(۱) أقول : كون الشيء عقليا كفوقية السماء يباين كونه فرضيا، فإن تحتية السماء ربما يفرض ، بل المقلى الذى يجب أن يحدث فى العقل إذا عقل العقل ذلك الشيء كفوقية السماء . وأما الفرضي فهو الذي يفرضه الفارض وإن كان محالا والذهن يشتملهما ، ويجب أن يفهم كل واحد منهما لئلا يقع بسبب الاشتباه غلط (۲) أقول : غير المتناهى لايصير متناهيا بنقصان كل شيء منه ، والتي ربما يكون متناهيا من وجه غير من وجه فلحقه خواص المتناهى من الوجه الأول وخواص غير المتناهى من الوجه الآخر ، وهذا كتضعيف الألف والألفين مرارا لانهاية لها فيكون أحد غير المتناهيين نصفا الآخر ، ولا يلزمه منه تناهى أحدهما. (۳) أقول : قد بينا أن الإضافة تعقل عند تصور المضافين والمتقدم والمتأخر موجودان في التصور معا ولا يلزم ذلك قيام موجود معدوم ، بل يلزم حدوث معقول متصور وذلك غير محال ، وقد عرفت أن ذلك ثابت فى نفس الأمر من غير الفرض وليس بالذهن الصرف.
In the essence itself, because the body as body is not said in relation to another, and as 'above' is said in relation to another, and because a thing may not be above then becomes above, so the essence remains in both states while the above-ness is not attained in both states. (1) Then Muammar, an early mutakallim, affirmed these relative accidents due to the strength of this argument and found no way to avoid the mentioned infinite regresses, so he accepted them and affirmed infinite accidents, each subsisting in another. The mutakallimun said: This is false, because every existing number has a half, and its half is less than the whole, and whatever is less than another is finite, so its half is finite in number, and whatever has a finite half is itself finite because it is double the finite. Muammar said: We do not concede that every number has a half; rather that is a property of finite numbers. Granted, but why did you say that whatever is less than another is finite? Are not Allah's powers less than His knowledge, and the doubling of a thousand an infinite number of times less than the doubling of two thousand an infinite number of times? (2) And we say: The philosophers' proof for affirming relations requires that the prior and the posterior be two existent attributes, and that is impossible, because the two relations exist together and their loci exist together, so the prior would exist with the posterior – this is a contradiction. And because we judge of yesterday in the present day that it is past, and the concept of its being past is not a negative matter because it became past after it was not past, so it is positive. And its positivity is not only in the mind, for if we assume the absence of assumption and consideration, that day is past in itself and is not the same as that day because when it was present it was not past. So it follows that the description of its being past is a real accident subsisting in it at the time of its non-existence, so an existent would subsist in a non-existent, which is impossible. (3) (1) I say: The thing being 'aqli like the above-ness of the sky differs from its being fardi; for the below-ness of the sky may be assumed. Rather the 'aqli is what must occur in the intellect when the intellect cognizes that thing, like the above-ness of the sky. As for the fardi, it is what the assumer assumes even if it is impossible, and the mind encompasses both. Each must be understood to avoid error due to confusion. (2) I say: The infinite does not become finite by subtracting something from it, and it may be finite in one respect and infinite in another, so it has the properties of the finite in the first respect and the properties of the infinite in the other respect. This is like the doubling of a thousand and two thousand an infinite number of times, so one of the two infinites is half of the other, and it does not necessitate the finitude of either. (3) I say: We have shown that relation is conceived when the two relata are conceived, and the prior and posterior exist in conception together, and that does not entail an existent subsisting in a non-existent; rather it entails the occurrence of an intelligible thing conceived, and that is not impossible. And you have known that this is established in the very matter without assumption, and it is not merely mental.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument. First, a proof is given that relational attributes like 'above-ness' are not real existent attributes because the essence remains unchanged while the relation changes. Second, Muammar's objection is presented: he accepted infinite accidents to avoid infinite regress. The mutakallimun refute him by arguing that every finite number has a half, and an infinite series would not have such halves. Muammar counters that not every number has a half, and that smaller infinities exist. Third, the author presents a direct argument against the philosophers' proof for relations, showing that it would require an accident to subsist in a non-existent (like the past day being described as past while it no longer exists), which is impossible. The marginal notes clarify key concepts: the distinction between 'aqli (intellectual) and fardi (assumptive) entities; the nature of the infinite (that one infinite can be half of another without becoming finite); and the defense that relations are only conceived in the mind and do not imply real accidents subsisting in non-existents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the philosophers' proof for relations is sound and that relations like pastness are real.
Correction: Razi shows that such a view leads to absurdity, and that relations are merely mental concepts.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd conclusion Razi derives from the philosophers' proof about yesterday being past?
A) That yesterday never existed.
B) That the pastness of yesterday would be an accident subsisting in a non-existent.
C) That pastness is a negative concept.
D) That yesterday is present.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument for real relations leads to absurdity; relations are mental concepts, not real accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often divide time into past, present, and future as if they are real containers. But this lesson shows that 'pastness' cannot be a real quality of anything. When you grieve over past sins or regret missed opportunities, are you attributing a reality to the past that it does not have? How might this shift your heart from dwelling on what is gone to trusting the All-Knowing who holds all times in His knowledge?
?
The philosophers argued that relations are real accidents, but Razi exposed the absurdity: a quality cannot subsist in nothing. Consider your own attachments—to status, wealth, relationships. Are you treating these as real attributes of yourself, as if they inhere in your essence? What would it mean to see them as mere mental considerations (iʿtibārī) that do not define your true self before Allāh?
?
If 'priority' and 'posteriority' are only mental concepts, then the temporal distance between you and the Prophet ﷺ is not a real barrier. Your connection to the Book and Sunnah is immediate in the mind. When you study these lessons, do you approach them as a remote historical text, or as a living address to your soul right now? What would change if you read every verse and ḥadīth as if it were revealed directly to you today?

The Nature of Position (Wad')
Razi distinguishes two meanings of position and asserts the impossibility of the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of position (wad') as used in kalam and why it cannot be a single accident inhering in an aggregate of parts.

Core Idea
Position is not a single accident spread over many parts; it is only individual relations.

In this lesson, we explore the concept of position (wad') as discussed by Imam Razi. Position, such as the posture of sitting, is often considered an accidental quality. Razi distinguishes between two interpretations: one that is unproblematic and another that leads to logical impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully.
Notice the two distinct meanings.
2
Identify the two meanings
Find where Razi states the uncontroversial meaning and the problematic one.
3
Understand the impossibility
Why is it impossible for one accident to inhere in multiple loci? Reflect on the principle.
This is a fundamental kalam principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوضع وهو كهيئة الجلوس مثلا فإن أريد به ما لكل واحد من آخر الجسم من الاين وماسة الغير فلا نزاع فى ثبوته ، وإن عنى به أمر وراء ذلك قائم بمجموع الأجزاء فهو محال لاستحالة حلول الواحد في المحال الكثيرة . لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال إنه عرضت لمجموع تلك الأجزاء وحدة باعتبارها صارت واحدة ، وحينئذ لا يلزم من قيام هيئة الوضع بها قيام الواحد بأكثر من الواحد . هو متناء
لانا نقول الإشكال في كيفية قيام تلك الوحدة بها كالإشكال في قيام هيئة الوضع بها ، فإن كان بسبب وحدة أخرى سابقة لزم التسلسل وكذا القول في الملك (1)
(1) أقول : الهيئة المسماة بالوضع إنما تحصل في الأجزاء بعد صيرورتها جملة واحدة ، وكذلك الزاوية والشكل وليس لك حلول العرض الواحد فى محال كثيرة ، إنما هو حلول عرض واحد في محل واحد ينقسم باعتبار غير اعتبار وحدته ولم يدل على استحالة ذلك دليلي ، وأما الوحدة فهى التي تجعل المجموع واحدا ، وإذا اعتبر فيه عدم الانقسام بوجه ما مثلا كمشرة فإنها لا تنقسم من حيث هى عشرة ، وإن انقسمت من حيث هي آحاد هي أجزاء العشرة ، وقد تتكرر الوحدة حين يقال وحدة واحدة، ولا يلزم منه ثبوته ، فإن موضع الوحدة الأولى هو الشيء الذي يقال إنه واحد ، وموضوع الوحدة الثانية هو الوحدة الأولى ، وإذا لم تتكرر الموضوعات فى مرتبة واحدة لم يحصل من الوحدات عدد ، وليس قيام الوحدة بالموضوع المنقسم محتاجا إلى هی وحدة تسبقها ، بل هى اعتبار عدم الانقسام فيها من حيث اعتبار كونها ذلك المجموع ولا يلزم التسلسل .
As for position (al-wad'), such as the posture of sitting, if what is meant by it is what each part has of location and contact with another, then there is no dispute about its existence. But if it means something beyond that subsisting in the aggregate of parts, then it is impossible because it is impossible for one [accident] to inhere in multiple loci. It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible that a unity arises in that aggregate of parts by virtue of which they become one? In that case, the inherence of the posture in them would not entail the inherence of one in more than one. It is finite. [But] we say: The difficulty concerning how that unity inheres in them is like the difficulty concerning the inherence of the posture in them. If it is due to another preceding unity, an infinite regress follows. The same applies to dominion (al-mulk). (1) (1) I say: The configuration called position (al-wad') only occurs in the parts after they become a single whole, and likewise the angle and the shape. It is not a case of one accident inhering in multiple loci; rather it is one accident inhering in a single locus that is divisible in a respect other than the respect of its unity. No proof has indicated its impossibility. As for unity, it is what makes the collection one. If one considers in it a certain indivisibility, like ten, it does not divide qua ten, although it divides qua units that are parts of ten. Unity can be repeated when one says 'one unity', but that does not entail its existence. For the locus of the first unity is the thing that is said to be one, and the subject of the second unity is the first unity. If subjects are not repeated in one rank, no number arises from the unities. The inherence of unity in a divisible subject does not require a preceding unity; rather it is the consideration of its indivisibility under the consideration of its being that collection, and no infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage defines and discusses the attribute of position (wad'). First, a distinction is made: if position means the individual location and contact of each part, it is uncontroversial. But if it means an accident that inheres in the whole aggregate of parts, that is impossible because one accident cannot inhere in multiple loci. An objection is raised: what if the parts acquire a unity, making them one locus? The author responds that the problem repeats: how does that unity inhere? If it requires another unity, an infinite regress ensues. The marginal note clarifies that position and similar accidents inhere in a single locus that is considered a unity, not in multiple disconnected parts. Unity itself is a mental consideration of indivisibility and does not need a preceding unity. The example of ten shows that a collection can be considered one without actual unity.

Key Terms
الوضع
الهيئة
المحل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Position is a single accident that inheres in the whole body as one.
Correction: Position is only the sum of individual relations; a single accident cannot inhere in multiple loci.

Checkpoint
What does 'position' (wad') uncontroversially refer to?
A) A single accident in the whole.
B) The location and contact of each part.
C) The shape of the body.
D) An attribute of the soul.

Key Takeaway
Position, if understood as a single accident in the aggregate, is impossible because one accident cannot inhere in many loci.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own body, do you think of 'sitting' as a single state of the whole body or as the sum of each part's position? After learning that a single accident cannot inhere in multiple loci, how must you revise your understanding of everyday postures?
?
This lesson argues that what appears to be a single quality over a composite is actually just individual relations. What are some other 'unified' qualities in your life that might actually be aggregations? How does this change the way you perceive yourself?
?
Are you ever tempted to attribute a single accident to a composite without considering the parts? In what area of your life might this lead to error? Commit to examining one such assumption this week.

The Unity Objection and Infinite Regress
Razi refutes the objection that unity solves the problem of position's inherence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that unity solves the problem of position and why Razi refutes it using the infinite regress argument.

Core Idea
Unity cannot rescue position because the inherence of unity itself faces the same problem.

After establishing the impossibility of position as a single accident in the aggregate, Razi addresses a possible objection: what if the aggregate acquires a unity, making it a single locus? He argues that this merely shifts the problem to how unity itself inheres. The marginal note further clarifies that unity is a mental consideration and does not require a preceding unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's proposal that unity solves the problem.
2
Trace the infinite regress
Follow Razi's response: the same difficulty applies to unity itself.
If unity needs another unity, we have regress.
3
Study the marginal note
The note clarifies that unity is a mental consideration, and position inheres in a single unified whole.
The example of ten helps understand unity as indivisibility in a respect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوضع وهو كهيئة الجلوس مثلا فإن أريد به ما لكل واحد من آخر الجسم من الاين وماسة الغير فلا نزاع فى ثبوته ، وإن عنى به أمر وراء ذلك قائم بمجموع الأجزاء فهو محال لاستحالة حلول الواحد في المحال الكثيرة . لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال إنه عرضت لمجموع تلك الأجزاء وحدة باعتبارها صارت واحدة ، وحينئذ لا يلزم من قيام هيئة الوضع بها قيام الواحد بأكثر من الواحد . هو متناء
لانا نقول الإشكال في كيفية قيام تلك الوحدة بها كالإشكال في قيام هيئة الوضع بها ، فإن كان بسبب وحدة أخرى سابقة لزم التسلسل وكذا القول في الملك (1)
(1) أقول : الهيئة المسماة بالوضع إنما تحصل في الأجزاء بعد صيرورتها جملة واحدة ، وكذلك الزاوية والشكل وليس لك حلول العرض الواحد فى محال كثيرة ، إنما هو حلول عرض واحد في محل واحد ينقسم باعتبار غير اعتبار وحدته ولم يدل على استحالة ذلك دليلي ، وأما الوحدة فهى التي تجعل المجموع واحدا ، وإذا اعتبر فيه عدم الانقسام بوجه ما مثلا كمشرة فإنها لا تنقسم من حيث هى عشرة ، وإن انقسمت من حيث هي آحاد هي أجزاء العشرة ، وقد تتكرر الوحدة حين يقال وحدة واحدة، ولا يلزم منه ثبوته ، فإن موضع الوحدة الأولى هو الشيء الذي يقال إنه واحد ، وموضوع الوحدة الثانية هو الوحدة الأولى ، وإذا لم تتكرر الموضوعات فى مرتبة واحدة لم يحصل من الوحدات عدد ، وليس قيام الوحدة بالموضوع المنقسم محتاجا إلى هی وحدة تسبقها ، بل هى اعتبار عدم الانقسام فيها من حيث اعتبار كونها ذلك المجموع ولا يلزم التسلسل .
As for position (al-wad'), such as the posture of sitting, if what is meant by it is what each part has of location and contact with another, then there is no dispute about its existence. But if it means something beyond that subsisting in the aggregate of parts, then it is impossible because it is impossible for one [accident] to inhere in multiple loci. It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible that a unity arises in that aggregate of parts by virtue of which they become one? In that case, the inherence of the posture in them would not entail the inherence of one in more than one. It is finite. [But] we say: The difficulty concerning how that unity inheres in them is like the difficulty concerning the inherence of the posture in them. If it is due to another preceding unity, an infinite regress follows. The same applies to dominion (al-mulk). (1) (1) I say: The configuration called position (al-wad') only occurs in the parts after they become a single whole, and likewise the angle and the shape. It is not a case of one accident inhering in multiple loci; rather it is one accident inhering in a single locus that is divisible in a respect other than the respect of its unity. No proof has indicated its impossibility. As for unity, it is what makes the collection one. If one considers in it a certain indivisibility, like ten, it does not divide qua ten, although it divides qua units that are parts of ten. Unity can be repeated when one says 'one unity', but that does not entail its existence. For the locus of the first unity is the thing that is said to be one, and the subject of the second unity is the first unity. If subjects are not repeated in one rank, no number arises from the unities. The inherence of unity in a divisible subject does not require a preceding unity; rather it is the consideration of its indivisibility under the consideration of its being that collection, and no infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage defines and discusses the attribute of position (wad'). First, a distinction is made: if position means the individual location and contact of each part, it is uncontroversial. But if it means an accident that inheres in the whole aggregate of parts, that is impossible because one accident cannot inhere in multiple loci. An objection is raised: what if the parts acquire a unity, making them one locus? The author responds that the problem repeats: how does that unity inhere? If it requires another unity, an infinite regress ensues. Unity itself is a mental consideration of indivisibility and does not need a preceding unity. The example of ten shows that a collection can be considered one without actual unity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity is a real accident that can inhere in the aggregate to make it a single locus.
Correction: Unity is a mental consideration; it does not require a preceding unity and does not entail infinite regress.

Checkpoint
According to the marginal note, what does unity (al-wahda) do?
A) It makes the collection one, but is only a mental consideration.
B) It is a real accident inhering in the parts.
C) It is the same as position.
D) It is divisible into parts.

Key Takeaway
Unity does not solve the problem of position because it is a mental consideration, not a real accident, and the inherence of position is actually in a single unified whole.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a belief you hold strongly. Have you ever checked whether the foundation of that belief leads to a regress that you have ignored? What practical step can you take today to examine the first principle of that belief?
?
The passage shows that the objector's proposed solution (unity) fails because it faces the same difficulty. Are there 'solutions' in your own life—emotional crutches, intellectual shortcuts—that actually suffer from the same problems they are meant to fix? Name one and commit to addressing it with sincere introspection.
?
The author treats unity as a mental consideration, not a real thing. In your relationship with Allah, are there concepts you treat as real (like 'my righteousness') that are merely mental constructs, preventing you from seeing the deeper reality of His mercy and your need? How can you align your understanding with the truth?

The Fourth Argument: Time as Measure of Existence and Its Refutation
Imam Razi refutes the argument that time cannot measure absolute existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's refutation of the argument that time cannot measure both changing and unchanging existents, and how the theologians resolve the issue.

Core Idea
Time cannot measure absolute existence, but theologians accept God's existence in measured times.

Imam Razi presents a fourth argument against the existence of time as a measure of absolute existence. He shows that if time existed, it would have to measure both changing and unchanging beings, which leads to a contradiction. However, he then addresses an objection and clarifies that the theologians (mutakallimūn) have a way to reconcile this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the argument's flow come through before interpreting.
Pay attention to the logical steps: argument, contradiction, objection, response.
2
Identify the core argument
Find where Imam Razi states the fourth argument: if time exists, it would measure absolute existence. Understand the necessity claim about God's existence yesterday, now, and tomorrow.
3
Understand the contradiction
Notice why time cannot measure absolute existence: if time is changing it cannot apply to the stable; if stable it cannot apply to the changing. This is the refutation.
This is the heart of the argument against time as a distinct entity.
4
Examine the objection and response
Read the objection that introduces zamān, dahr, and sarmad. See how Imam Razi attributes this view to Shaykh Abī al-Barakāt and concludes that theologians accept the eternal in measured times without contradiction.
This shows that the issue is resolved through terminological distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها: لو كان الزمان موجودا لكان مقدار المطلق الوجود، فإنا كما نعلم بالضرورة أن من الحركات ما كانت موجودة أمس أو منها ما يوجد غدا، كذلك تعلم بالضرورة أن الله تعالى كان موجودا بالأمس، وأنه موجود الآن وسيبقى موجودا غدا فإن جاز إنكار أحدهما جاز إنكار الآخر، لكن يستحيل أن يكون مقدارا للمطلق الموجود، لأنه في نفسه إن كان متجددا استحال انطباقه على الثابت، وإن كان ثابتا استحال انطباقه على المتغير. فإن قلت: نسبة التغير إلى المتغير هو الزمان ونسبته إلى الثابت هو الدهر ونسبة الثابت إلى الثابت هو السرمد. أقول: القول بأن الزمان مقدار الوجود قول الشيخ أبي البركات فإنه يقول: الباقي لا يتصور بقاؤه إلا في زمان مستمر، وما لا يكون في الزمان ويكون باقيا لابد وأن يكون لبقائه مقدار من الزمان، فالزمان مقدار الوجود، والمتكلمون حيث قالوا القديم موجود في أزمنة مقدرة لا نهاية لها فقد حكموا بصحة انطباق الثابت على المتغير ولم يقتض ذلك محالا.
The fourth: if time were existent, it would be the measure of absolute existence. For just as we know by necessity that some motions existed yesterday or some exist tomorrow, so too we know by necessity that God Most High existed yesterday, and that He exists now, and will continue to exist tomorrow. If it is allowed to deny one, it is allowed to deny the other. But it is impossible for it to be a measure of absolute existence, because in itself, if it is changing, its application to the stable is impossible; and if it is stable, its application to the changing is impossible. If you say: the relation of change to the changing is time (zamān), its relation to the stable is dahr (eternity), and the relation of the stable to the stable is sarmad (everlasting). I say: The statement that time is the measure of existence is the statement of Shaykh Abī al-Barakāt. For he says: The enduring thing cannot be conceived to endure except in continuous time; and what is not in time but is enduring must have for its endurance a quantity of time. So time is the measure of existence. And the theologians (al-mutakallimūn), when they say that the eternal (al-qadīm) exists in measured times with no end, they have judged the validity of applying the stable to the changing, and that does not necessitate an impossibility.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the fourth argument, which claims that if time exists, it must measure both changing and unchanging existents (like God), but that is impossible. The objection introduces the terms zamān (time for change), dahr (eternity for unchanging relative to change), and sarmad (everlasting for unchanging relative to unchanging). The response cites Abī al-Barakāt's view that time is the measure of existence itself, and the theologians accept the application of measured times to the eternal without contradiction.

Key Terms
الزمان (al-zamān): time
الدهر (al-dahr): eternity
السرمد (al-sarmad): everlasting
مقدار الوجود (miqdār al-wujūd): measure of existence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that time cannot apply to God at all.
Correction: The theologians accept that God exists in measured times (azmina muqaddara) without end, so the stable can be related to time via dahr and sarmad.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what is the position of the theologians (al-mutakallimūn) regarding the eternal (al-qadīm) and time?
A) The eternal cannot be in time at all
B) The eternal exists in measured times with no end
C) Time only applies to bodies
D) The eternal is beyond any relation to time

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the fourth argument by showing that time cannot measure absolute existence, but theologians reconcile it by accepting that the eternal exists in measured times without ending.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that God existed yesterday, exists now, and will exist tomorrow. But do you truly live with the awareness that every moment of your life is measured by a time that relates to Him? In what specific ways does that reality change how you approach each hour of this day?
?
Imam Razi shows that time cannot measure God's absolute existence, yet the theologians accept that God exists in measured times without end. Does your heart rest in the stillness of His eternal 'now', or do you still measure His faithfulness by the changing circumstances of your life? What would it take for you to trust Him as the Stable One in the midst of your changes?
?
The terms zaman, dahr, and sarmad distinguish how God relates to change and stability. When you look at your own life—the constants and the transformations—which of these 'times' do you most need to remember today? Will you actively meditate on that relation to deepen your worship?

Defining the Essence of Life
Definition of life and majority's claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that life cannot be reduced to physical qualities like temperamental balance or sensory-motor power, and must be conceived as something else if it is to be a distinct attribute.

Core Idea
Life is not a physical property; it is a distinct attribute that enables action.

Razi begins by clarifying what 'life' might mean. If it equals temperament or sensory-motor power, it's intelligible; but if it is something else, we must first conceive it and then prove its existence. He also presents the majority's view that life is the attribute that allows an essence to know and act. This lesson sets the conceptual stage before moving to specific proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the essence of life
Read the Arabic text slowly, paying attention to the distinction between what is intelligible (balance, sensory power) and a possible third meaning.
Notice Razi’s careful approach: first conceptualization, then proof.
2
Understand the majority's claim
The majority argues that life is an attribute that makes an essence capable of knowledge and power. Examine their proof: without such an attribute, the living would have no priority over the inanimate.
This is a foundational kalam argument for distinct attributes.
3
Reflect on the conceptual framework
Ask yourself: why is it important to distinguish between a physical condition (like balance) and an added attribute (life)? How does this relate to understanding divine attributes?
This will connect to later lessons on God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان ماهية الحياة. اعلم أن المراد منها إن كان اعتدال المزاج أو قوة الحس والحركة فهو أمر معقول وإن كان شيئا ثالثا فلا بد من إعادة تصوره ثم إقامة الدليل على ثبوته. والجمهور زعموا أنها صفة لأجلها يصح على الذات أن يعلم وأن يقدر، واحتجوا بأنه لولا امتياز الحي عن الجماد بصفة وإلا لم يكن اتصاف الحى بصفة أن يعلم وأن يقدر أولى من الجماد. واحتج ابن سينا في القانون: بأن العضو المفلوج حى فحياته إما أن تكون قوة الحس والحركة أو قوة التغذية أو نوعا ثالثا والأول باطل، لأن العضو المفلوج ليس له قوة الحركة والحس، والثاني باطل لأن قوة التغذية قد تبطل مع بقاء العضو المفلوج حيا، ولأن القوة الغاذية حاصلة للنبات ولا حياة له فثبت أن الحياة أمر ثالث. والجواب عن الأول: معارضة بأنه لولا امتياز الذات الحية بما لأجله صح أن يصير حيا وإلا لم يكن بأن يصير حيا أولى من غيره، وهذا يقتضى اشتراط الحياة بحياة أخرى وكل ما هو جوابهم هناك فهو جوابنا هاهنا. وعن الثاني أن معنى كون العضو المفلوج حيا بقاء قوة التغذية قوله تبطل هذه القوة مع بقاء الحياة، قلنا لا نسلم فلم لا يجوز أن يقال القوة باقية لكنها عاجزة عن الفعل قوله الغاذية حاصلة في النبات قلنا أنت تساعدنا على أن غاذية النبات والحيوان مختلفان بالنوعية والماهية والمختلفان لا يجب اشتراكهما في الأحكام. أقول: قبل الأعراض التي لا يتصف بها غير الحى عشرة الحياة والعلم والقدرة والاعتقاد والظن والنظر والإرادة والكرامة والشهوة والنفرة، ولم يقل أحد أن اعتدال المزاج أو قوة الحس والحركة هو الحياة، بل قالوا إن الأول شرط في حصول الحياة للحيوان المركب من الأخلاط أو من الأركان، والثاني معلول للحياة. وقوله في المعارضة: وهذا يقتضى اشتراط الحياة بحياة أخرى، ليس بشيء لأنه يقتضى اشتراط الحياة بمخصص هو الاعتدال في الحيوانات، ومن أين لزم أن يكون ذلك المخصص صفة أخرى. وقوله في النقل عن ابن سينا: إن قوة التغذية قد تبطل مع بقاء العضو المفلوج حيا، فزيادة المفلوج غير محتاج إليها لأن قوة التغذية قد تبطل مع بقاء العضو حيا كالعضو النابل، وقوله ولم لا يجوز أن تكون القوة
Statement of the essence of life. Know that if what is meant by it is temperamental balance or the power of sensation and motion, then it is an intelligible matter, but if it is a third thing, then one must reconsider its conception and then establish proof of its existence. The majority claimed that it is an attribute because of which it is valid for the essence to know and to have power. They argued: if the living thing were not distinguished from the inanimate by an attribute, then the living thing's being described as able to know and have power would not be more fitting than the inanimate. Ibn Sina argued in al-Qanun: 'The paralyzed limb is living, so its life is either the power of sensation and motion, or the power of nutrition, or a third type. The first is invalid because the paralyzed limb has no power of motion and sensation. The second is invalid because the power of nutrition may cease while the paralyzed limb remains living, and because the nutritive power exists in plants and they have no life. Thus it is established that life is a third thing.' The answer to the first [argument]: a counter-argument that if the living essence were not distinguished by that for which it is valid to become living, then it would not be more fitting to become living than another. This necessitates the condition of life by another life, and whatever their answer is there, our answer is here. As for the second [argument]: the meaning of the paralyzed limb's being living is the remaining of the nutritive power. He says this power ceases while life remains. We say: we do not accept that. Why is it not possible that the power remains but is unable to act? He says: the nutritive power exists in plants. We say: you help us that the nutritive power of plants and animals differs in species and essence, and differing things are not necessarily required to share ruling. I say: The attributes that are not described except by the living are ten: life, knowledge, power, belief, opinion, consideration, will, honor, appetite, and aversion. No one has said that temperamental balance or the power of sensation and motion is life. Rather, they said the first is a condition for the occurrence of life in the animal composed of humors or elements, and the second is an effect of life. And his statement in the counter-argument: 'this necessitates the condition of life by another life'—it is nothing, because it necessitates the condition of life by a specificator which is balance in animals. Where does it follow that that specificator must be another attribute? And his statement in the transmission from Ibn Sina: 'the nutritive power may cease while the paralyzed limb remains living'—the addition of 'paralyzed' is unnecessary because the nutritive power may cease while the limb remains living, like the wasted limb. And his statement: 'why is it not possible that the power...'
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and argumentative discussion of the essence of life (mahiyyat al-hayah). The author begins by clarifying what is meant: if life is interpreted as temperamental balance or the power of sensation and motion, it is intelligible; but if it is something else, one must conceive it and then provide proof. The majority (al-jumhur) hold that life is an attribute that enables the essence to know and have power. Their proof is that without a distinguishing attribute, the living would have no priority over the inanimate in being described with knowledge and power. Ibn Sina argues in al-Qanun that the paralyzed limb is alive, so its life must be either sensory-motor power, nutritive power, or a third thing. He rejects the first because the paralyzed limb lacks sensation and motion, and rejects the second because nutritive power may cease while the limb remains alive, and because plants have nutritive power without life. Thus life is a third distinct thing. The author provides answers: to the first argument, he offers a counter-argument that if the living essence were not distinguished by something for which it becomes living, then it would have no priority—this would lead to an infinite regress of requiring life for life, and the answer is the same as theirs. To the second, he argues that the meaning of 'living' for the paralyzed limb is that nutritive power remains, and he questions whether it ceases; it may remain but be unable to act. He also points out that plant and animal nutritive powers differ in species, so they need not share the same ruling. In his marginal comment, he lists the ten attributes specific to the living, and clarifies that no one claims temperance or sensory-motor power is life itself; rather, temperance is a condition for life in composite animals, and sensory-motor power is an effect of life. He also criticizes the claim that the counter-argument leads to requiring another life, noting that the specificator is balance, not another attribute. He then points out that Ibn Sina's example of the paralyzed limb is redundant because even a non-paralyzed limb can lose nutritive power while remaining alive (like a wasted limb).

Key Terms
الحياة
الماهية
اعتدال المزاج
قوة الحس والحركة
الذات
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think life is simply a physical property like balanced temperament or ability to move.
Correction: Razi shows that if life were only physical, it would be intelligible, but the majority argues it is an added attribute; the implication is that divine life cannot be physical.

Checkpoint
According to the majority, what does the attribute of life enable the essence to do?
A) To grow and reproduce
B) To know and have power
C) To move and sense
D) To have a balanced temperament

Key Takeaway
Life is an additional attribute that distinguishes the living from the inanimate and enables knowledge and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every time you act with knowledge or power, you are exercising the attribute of life that Allah has uniquely given you. Are you using your life today as a trusting steward, or are you squandering it on what does not please your Creator?
?
If a stone cannot know or act, but you can, what does that say about the trust (amānah) you carry? What one decision today will reflect that you truly understand this responsibility?
?
The majority argue that life is an attribute enabling action, not merely a physical property. In what area of your life are you acting as if your capabilities are merely biological, ignoring the spiritual dimension? Right now, commit to one act that acknowledges life as a divine gift.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
Ibn Sina's Argument from the Paralyzed Limb
Ibn Sina's argument for life as a distinct attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that life must be a distinct attribute, not reducible to sensory-motor power or nutritive power, using the example of a paralyzed limb.

Core Idea
A paralyzed limb shows life cannot be sensation or nutrition; it must be something else.

Ibn Sina, in his medical work al-Qanun, presents a simple but powerful argument. A paralyzed limb is still alive, yet it lacks both sensation/motion and sometimes even nutrition. Therefore, life cannot be identified with either of these powers; it must be a third, distinct thing. This lesson unpacks his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's argument carefully
Read the Arabic text of the argument, noting the three options he presents and how he eliminates two.
Focus on the logical structure: disjunction, elimination, conclusion.
2
Understand the reasoning for rejecting each option
First option: sensory-motor power – rejected because the paralyzed limb lacks sensation and motion. Second: nutritive power – rejected because it can cease while the limb remains alive, and because plants have it without life.
The plant example is key: nutrition exists without life, so it cannot be the essence of life.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Ibn Sina concludes that life must be a third type, an attribute distinct from these powers.
This supports the majority view that life is a distinct attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان ماهية الحياة. اعلم أن المراد منها إن كان اعتدال المزاج أو قوة الحس والحركة فهو أمر معقول وإن كان شيئا ثالثا فلا بد من إعادة تصوره ثم إقامة الدليل على ثبوته. والجمهور زعموا أنها صفة لأجلها يصح على الذات أن يعلم وأن يقدر، واحتجوا بأنه لولا امتياز الحي عن الجماد بصفة وإلا لم يكن اتصاف الحى بصفة أن يعلم وأن يقدر أولى من الجماد. واحتج ابن سينا في القانون: بأن العضو المفلوج حى فحياته إما أن تكون قوة الحس والحركة أو قوة التغذية أو نوعا ثالثا والأول باطل، لأن العضو المفلوج ليس له قوة الحركة والحس، والثاني باطل لأن قوة التغذية قد تبطل مع بقاء العضو المفلوج حيا، ولأن القوة الغاذية حاصلة للنبات ولا حياة له فثبت أن الحياة أمر ثالث. والجواب عن الأول: معارضة بأنه لولا امتياز الذات الحية بما لأجله صح أن يصير حيا وإلا لم يكن بأن يصير حيا أولى من غيره، وهذا يقتضى اشتراط الحياة بحياة أخرى وكل ما هو جوابهم هناك فهو جوابنا هاهنا. وعن الثاني أن معنى كون العضو المفلوج حيا بقاء قوة التغذية قوله تبطل هذه القوة مع بقاء الحياة، قلنا لا نسلم فلم لا يجوز أن يقال القوة باقية لكنها عاجزة عن الفعل قوله الغاذية حاصلة في النبات قلنا أنت تساعدنا على أن غاذية النبات والحيوان مختلفان بالنوعية والماهية والمختلفان لا يجب اشتراكهما في الأحكام. أقول: قبل الأعراض التي لا يتصف بها غير الحى عشرة الحياة والعلم والقدرة والاعتقاد والظن والنظر والإرادة والكرامة والشهوة والنفرة، ولم يقل أحد أن اعتدال المزاج أو قوة الحس والحركة هو الحياة، بل قالوا إن الأول شرط في حصول الحياة للحيوان المركب من الأخلاط أو من الأركان، والثاني معلول للحياة. وقوله في المعارضة: وهذا يقتضى اشتراط الحياة بحياة أخرى، ليس بشيء لأنه يقتضى اشتراط الحياة بمخصص هو الاعتدال في الحيوانات، ومن أين لزم أن يكون ذلك المخصص صفة أخرى. وقوله في النقل عن ابن سينا: إن قوة التغذية قد تبطل مع بقاء العضو المفلوج حيا، فزيادة المفلوج غير محتاج إليها لأن قوة التغذية قد تبطل مع بقاء العضو حيا كالعضو النابل، وقوله ولم لا يجوز أن تكون القوة

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You define your worth by what you do and feel—your career, relationships, emotions. But this lesson shows that life itself is distinct from all action and sensation. If you lost every ability to move or perceive, would you still know you are alive? Would you still thank Allah for the mere pulse of existence? Commit to spending one silent minute today recognizing the gift of life independent of any function.
?
Ibn Sina uses a paralyzed limb to prove life remains even when power is gone. Think of a part of your life that feels 'paralyzed'—a spiritual discipline that has stalled, a relationship that seems dead, a habit you cannot break. Are you tempted to pronounce it dead? What would it mean to acknowledge the hidden life still present in that area, waiting for the power to return? How would that change your dua? This week, ask Allah to restore the function, but first thank Him for the life that remains.
?
The argument strips away everything we usually associate with life—sensation, movement, growth—and says life is something else. What is that 'something else' for you? Is it your soul? Your consciousness? Your connection to Allah? If you cannot define it, perhaps you have never truly contemplated what it means to be alive. Before you sleep tonight, write your own definition of life based on this lesson, and let it change how you see yourself and others.

Razi's Refutation and Clarification
Razi's refutations and clarifications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's responses to the arguments of the majority and Ibn Sina, and his clarification that balance is a condition and sensory-motor power an effect of life, not life itself.

Core Idea
The counter-arguments fail; life is a distinct attribute with balance as condition and sensory power as effect.

After presenting the proofs, Razi offers his own commentary. He counters the majority's argument with a parallel that would lead to infinite regress if taken literally. He also criticizes Ibn Sina's paralyzed limb example, suggesting that nutritive power may remain but be inactive, and that plant and animal nutrition are different. Finally, he lists the ten attributes exclusive to the living and clarifies the relationship between life, balance, and sensory-motor power.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's response to the majority
Razi offers a counter-argument: if a living essence needs an attribute to be living, then that attribute would itself need another attribute, leading to infinite regress. But he dismisses this by noting that the condition is balance, not another life.
Understand the structure of the objection and its rebuttal.
2
Examine his critique of Ibn Sina
Razi questions whether nutritive power truly ceases; it may only be unable to act. Also, plant nutrition differs from animal nutrition, so the plant argument does not work.
Focus on the subtlety of the response.
3
Study the list of attributes and final clarifications
Razi lists ten attributes that only living beings can have, and reiterates that balance is a condition for life in composite animals, not life itself, and sensory-motor power is an effect of life.
This solidifies the lesson: life is a distinct attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان ماهية الحياة. اعلم أن المراد منها إن كان اعتدال المزاج أو قوة الحس والحركة فهو أمر معقول وإن كان شيئا ثالثا فلا بد من إعادة تصوره ثم إقامة الدليل على ثبوته. والجمهور زعموا أنها صفة لأجلها يصح على الذات أن يعلم وأن يقدر، واحتجوا بأنه لولا امتياز الحي عن الجماد بصفة وإلا لم يكن اتصاف الحى بصفة أن يعلم وأن يقدر أولى من الجماد. واحتج ابن سينا في القانون: بأن العضو المفلوج حى فحياته إما أن تكون قوة الحس والحركة أو قوة التغذية أو نوعا ثالثا والأول باطل، لأن العضو المفلوج ليس له قوة الحركة والحس، والثاني باطل لأن قوة التغذية قد تبطل مع بقاء العضو المفلوج حيا، ولأن القوة الغاذية حاصلة للنبات ولا حياة له فثبت أن الحياة أمر ثالث. والجواب عن الأول: معارضة بأنه لولا امتياز الذات الحية بما لأجله صح أن يصير حيا وإلا لم يكن بأن يصير حيا أولى من غيره، وهذا يقتضى اشتراط الحياة بحياة أخرى وكل ما هو جوابهم هناك فهو جوابنا هاهنا. وعن الثاني أن معنى كون العضو المفلوج حيا بقاء قوة التغذية قوله تبطل هذه القوة مع بقاء الحياة، قلنا لا نسلم فلم لا يجوز أن يقال القوة باقية لكنها عاجزة عن الفعل قوله الغاذية حاصلة في النبات قلنا أنت تساعدنا على أن غاذية النبات والحيوان مختلفان بالنوعية والماهية والمختلفان لا يجب اشتراكهما في الأحكام. أقول: قبل الأعراض التي لا يتصف بها غير الحى عشرة الحياة والعلم والقدرة والاعتقاد والظن والنظر والإرادة والكرامة والشهوة والنفرة، ولم يقل أحد أن اعتدال المزاج أو قوة الحس والحركة هو الحياة، بل قالوا إن الأول شرط في حصول الحياة للحيوان المركب من الأخلاط أو من الأركان، والثاني معلول للحياة. وقوله في المعارضة: وهذا يقتضى اشتراط الحياة بحياة أخرى، ليس بشيء لأنه يقتضى اشتراط الحياة بمخصص هو الاعتدال في الحيوانات، ومن أين لزم أن يكون ذلك المخصص صفة أخرى. وقوله في النقل عن ابن سينا: إن قوة التغذية قد تبطل مع بقاء العضو المفلوج حيا، فزيادة المفلوج غير محتاج إليها لأن قوة التغذية قد تبطل مع بقاء العضو حيا كالعضو النابل، وقوله ولم لا يجوز أن تكون القوة
Statement of the essence of life. Know that if what is meant by it is temperamental balance or the power of sensation and motion, then it is an intelligible matter, but if it is a third thing, then one must reconsider its conception and then establish proof of its existence. The majority claimed that it is an attribute because of which it is valid for the essence to know and to have power. They argued: if the living thing were not distinguished from the inanimate by an attribute, then the living thing's being described as able to know and have power would not be more fitting than the inanimate. Ibn Sina argued in al-Qanun: 'The paralyzed limb is living, so its life is either the power of sensation and motion, or the power of nutrition, or a third type. The first is invalid because the paralyzed limb has no power of motion and sensation. The second is invalid because the power of nutrition may cease while the paralyzed limb remains living, and because the nutritive power exists in plants and they have no life. Thus it is established that life is a third thing.' The answer to the first [argument]: a counter-argument that if the living essence were not distinguished by that for which it is valid to become living, then it would not be more fitting to become living than another. This necessitates the condition of life by another life, and whatever their answer is there, our answer is here. As for the second [argument]: the meaning of the paralyzed limb's being living is the remaining of the nutritive power. He says this power ceases while life remains. We say: we do not accept that. Why is it not possible that the power remains but is unable to act? He says: the nutritive power exists in plants. We say: you help us that the nutritive power of plants and animals differs in species and essence, and differing things are not necessarily required to share ruling. I say: The attributes that are not described except by the living are ten: life, knowledge, power, belief, opinion, consideration, will, honor, appetite, and aversion. No one has said that temperamental balance or the power of sensation and motion is life. Rather, they said the first is a condition for the occurrence of life in the animal composed of humors or elements, and the second is an effect of life. And his statement in the counter-argument: 'this necessitates the condition of life by another life'—it is nothing, because it necessitates the condition of life by a specificator which is balance in animals. Where does it follow that that specificator must be another attribute? And his statement in the transmission from Ibn Sina: 'the nutritive power may cease while the paralyzed limb remains living'—the addition of 'paralyzed' is unnecessary because the nutritive power may cease while the limb remains living, like the wasted limb. And his statement: 'why is it not possible that the power...'
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and argumentative discussion of the essence of life (mahiyyat al-hayah). The author begins by clarifying what is meant: if life is interpreted as temperamental balance or the power of sensation and motion, it is intelligible; but if it is something else, one must conceive it and then provide proof. The majority (al-jumhur) hold that life is an attribute that enables the essence to know and have power. Their proof is that without a distinguishing attribute, the living would have no priority over the inanimate in being described with knowledge and power. Ibn Sina argues in al-Qanun that the paralyzed limb is alive, so its life must be either sensory-motor power, nutritive power, or a third thing. He rejects the first because the paralyzed limb lacks sensation and motion, and rejects the second because nutritive power may cease while the limb remains alive, and because plants have nutritive power without life. Thus life is a third distinct thing. The author provides answers: to the first argument, he offers a counter-argument that if the living essence were not distinguished by something for which it becomes living, then it would have no priority—this would lead to an infinite regress of requiring life for life, and the answer is the same as theirs. To the second, he argues that the meaning of 'living' for the paralyzed limb is that nutritive power remains, and he questions whether it ceases; it may remain but be unable to act. He also points out that plant and animal nutritive powers differ in species, so they need not share the same ruling. In his marginal comment, he lists the ten attributes specific to the living, and clarifies that no one claims temperance or sensory-motor power is life itself; rather, temperance is a condition for life in composite animals, and sensory-motor power is an effect of life. He also criticizes the claim that the counter-argument leads to requiring another life, noting that the specificator is balance, not another attribute. He then points out that Ibn Sina's example of the paralyzed limb is redundant because even a non-paralyzed limb can lose nutritive power while remaining alive (like a wasted limb).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that life is identical to a balanced mixture of humors or to the ability to move and sense.
Correction: Razi explicitly states that balance is only a condition for life in certain animals, and sensory-motor power is an effect of life, not life itself.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Life is a distinct attribute; balance is a condition and sensory-motor power is an effect, not life itself.

Death as a Positive or Negative Attribute
Razi presents two positions on death as a positive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions on whether death is a positive attribute and the key arguments each side uses from the Quran.

Core Idea
Death in kalam: a real attribute or just absence of life?

In this passage from his Muhassal, Imam Razi presents a debated issue in kalam: is death a 'positive' (wujūdī) attribute — something that exists in itself — or is it merely the absence of life? The two sides each appeal to the same Quranic verse, but they interpret it differently. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the key concept of 'khalq' as estimation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Notice that the issue arises among those who affirm the attribute of life.
The passage assumes knowledge of the attribute of life.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage presents two groups. Find their claims and their evidence.
3
Analyze the key verse and interpretation
Understand how the verse 'He created death and life' is used by both sides, and the refutation based on 'khalq' meaning 'taqdīr'.
The verse is Q 67:2.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
This debate shows how seemingly straightforward Quranic verses can be interpreted differently in kalam, and how the framework of positive vs. negative attributes works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : القائلون بهذه الصفة وهى الحياة منهم من أثبت أن الموت صفة وجودية محتجا بقوله تعالى وخلق الموت والحياة ، ومنهم من لم يقل به وزعم أنه عبارة من عدم الحياة عما من شأنه أن يكون حيا، وأجاب عن التمسك بالآية بأن الخلق هو التقدير ولا يجب كون المقدور وجوديا .
Issue: Those who affirm this attribute, which is life, some of them affirmed that death is a positive attribute, arguing by His statement exalted 'And He created death and life,' and some of them did not say that and claimed that it is an expression of the absence of life from that which is apt to be alive, and they responded to the argument by the verse that creation (khalq) is estimation (taqdīr) and it is not necessary that the estimated thing be existent.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam issue: whether death is a positive (wujūdī) attribute or merely a negative (ʿadamī) term. Razi states that among those who affirm the attribute of life, some (like Abu Ali al-Jubbai) hold that death is a positive attribute, citing the Quranic verse 'He created death and life' (Q 67:2) — since creation implies bringing something into existence, death must be an existent thing. Others deny this, claiming that death is merely an expression for the absence of life in something capable of being alive. They respond to the proof from the verse by interpreting 'creation' (khalq) as 'estimation' (taqdīr), so it does not necessitate that what is estimated be an existent entity. Thus, Razi outlines the two positions without yet favoring one.

Key Terms
صفة وجودية
عدم الحياة
تقدير
الخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creation (khalq) always means bringing an existent thing into existence.
Correction: In kalam, khalq can mean estimation (taqdīr) and does not necessitate that the estimated object be an existent entity, as argued by those who deny death is a positive attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the argument of those who deny that death is a positive attribute based on the verse?
A) They say death is not mentioned in the verse.
B) They interpret 'khalq' as 'taqdīr' (estimation) so it does not require an existent thing.
C) They say that life and death are both negative.
D) They deny the verse altogether.

Key Takeaway
The debate over whether death is a positive attribute hinges on the interpretation of 'khalq' in the Quran, with one side seeing it as creation of an existent thing and the other as mere estimation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read about the debate on whether death is a created entity or merely the absence of life. But in your daily life, do you treat death as a real, inevitable event that will come to you, or do you live as if it is just an abstract concept that happens to others? How would your actions change today if you were certain that death is a positive attribute created by Allah and that you will meet it personally?
?
The verse 'He created death and life' is often recited, but have you ever paused to consider that death itself is deliberately created by Allah? If death is a creation with a purpose, what does that imply about your life's priorities? Are you using your time in a way that acknowledges the reality of both death and life as divine creations, or do you neglect one?
?
When you face hardships or the death of loved ones, do you find comfort in the belief that death is not just an end but a created entity under Allah's control? How can this theological insight transform your patience and trust in Allah's decree?

Critique of Defining Death as Absence of Life
Editor identifies proponent and critiques definition of death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of death as absence of life is problematic and who advocated for death as a positive attribute.

Core Idea
Death is not mere absence; it requires a preceding life.

This lesson examines an editorial note from Imam Razi's Muhaṣṣal, where he identifies the unique proponent of death as a positive attribute and critiques the common definition. The note clarifies a subtle conceptual distinction that affects how we understand divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent
Read the passage and note who holds that death is a positive attribute (thubūtī). The editor names Abu Ali al-Jubbai as the sole proponent.
2
Understand the definition being critiqued
The definition in question is 'death as the absence of life from that which is apt to be alive.' Recognize why this might seem plausible at first.
The editor argues this definition is not correct.
3
Grasp the counterexample of the fetus
The editor points out that if death were simply absence of life, a fetus before life enters it would be considered dead, which is absurd.
This shows that death conceptually requires that life preceded the absence.
4
Appreciate the logical flaw
The definition fails because it does not include the condition that life must have been present before the absence.
Thus, death is not a simple privation but a state that presupposes prior life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : القائل بكون الموت ثبوتيا هو أبو على الجبائي وحده، والعبارة عن الموت بعدم الحياة عمن من شأنه أن يكون حيا ليس بصحيح ، فإن الموت يدخل في مفهومه سبق الحياة على ذلك العدم إلا لكان الجنين عند قرب حلول الحياة فيه ميتا .
I say: The one who holds that death is positive is Abu Ali al-Jubbai alone, and the expression of death as the absence of life from that which is apt to be alive is not correct, for death includes in its concept the precedence of life over that absence, otherwise the fetus at the time of the near arrival of life in it would be dead.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This is an editorial note (marked by 'aqūl') that identifies the proponent of death as a positive attribute as Abu Ali al-Jubbai alone, and then criticizes the opposing definition (death as absence of life). The editor argues that 'death' conceptually requires that life preceded the absence; otherwise, a fetus just before life enters it would be called dead, which is absurd. Thus, the definition is flawed.

Key Terms
أبو على الجبائي
سبق الحياة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Death is merely the absence of life.
Correction: Death conceptually requires that life preceded the absence; otherwise, a fetus before life would be considered dead.

Checkpoint

A) Because death is a positive attribute by consensus.
B) Because death requires the precedence of life, otherwise a fetus before life would be dead.
C) Because the verse does not mention death.
D) Because life is not an attribute.

Key Takeaway
Death is not simply the absence of life; it must be preceded by life, otherwise the definition would absurdly label a pre-life fetus as dead.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Life Does Not Require Bodily Structure
Razi argues that life does not require a bodily structure, refuting Mu'tazila and philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that life does not need a bodily structure, using a logical dilemma to refute the Mu'tazila and philosophers.

Core Idea
Life can exist without a physical structure; otherwise, logical absurdities follow.

Razi presents a logical dilemma against those who claim that life requires a bodily structure. He shows that if the world had a single life, it would require an accident to be in multiple places, which is impossible. If each part has its own life, then life in one part would depend on life in another, leading to circularity. Therefore, structure is unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then the literal translation. Let Razi's own argument come through before interpreting.
Notice the concise style and logical structure.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities: either the entire world has one life, or each part has its own life. Identify each horn and its refutation.
3
Understand why each horn is impossible
First horn: a single accident cannot inhere in multiple loci. Second horn: leads to circular dependence (dawr) because parts are similar.
These are foundational kalam principles.
4
Conclude the argument
Since both options are impossible, the original claim that life requires structure is false. Life can exist without it, e.g., in God.
This conclusion has implications for divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : البنية ليست شرطا لوجود الحياة خلافا للمعتزلة والفلاسفة لما أن العالم بمجموع الأجزاء إما أن يكون له حياة واحدة أو القائم بكل جزء حياة على حدة ، والأول يقتضى حلول العرض الواحد فى المحال الكثيرة وهو محال ، وأما الثاني فمحال ، لأن الأجزاء متماثلة ، فلو توقف جواز قيام الحياة الجزء واحد على قيام حياة أخرى لجزء آخر لكان الأمر من الجانب الآخر كذلك ويلزم الدور وهو محال
Issue: Structure (bunya) is not a condition (sharṭ) for the existence of life, contrary to the Muʿtazila and the philosophers. For the world (al-ʿālam) consisting of the totality of parts either has one life, or each part has a separate life. The first entails the indwelling (ḥulūl) of a single accident (ʿaraḍ) in multiple loci (maḥall), which is impossible (muḥāl). As for the second, it is impossible because the parts are similar (mutamāthila); if the permissibility of life subsisting in one part were dependent on another life subsisting in another part, then the same would hold from the other side, leading to circularity (dawr), which is impossible.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage argues against the view that life requires a bodily structure (bunya) — a position held by the Muʿtazila and philosophers. Razi presents a dilemma: either the entire world of parts has a single life, or each part has its own life. The first option is impossible because an accident (like life) cannot inhere in multiple loci simultaneously. The second option is also impossible: since parts are similar, if life in one part depended on life in another, that would cause circular dependence (dawr). Therefore, structure is not a necessary condition; life can exist without it (e.g., in God, life is not tied to a body).

Key Terms
البنية
شرط
العرض
حلول
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Life inherently requires a physical body or structure.
Correction: Razi's argument shows that requiring structure leads to logical absurdities, so life can exist without it, as in the case of God.

Checkpoint
Why is the option of a single life inhering in multiple parts impossible?
A) Because life is not an accident.
B) Because an accident cannot inhere in multiple loci.
C) Because parts are dissimilar.
D) Because it would lead to circularity.

Key Takeaway
Structure is not a necessary condition for life; logical reasoning shows that requiring structure leads to impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
